  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_25e4bac8-31ee-4b2e-88a5-dea19c4b0534'

<span id="freeTextContainer4763168872098355559">Born Jalal ad-Din Mohammed Balkhi in Persia early in the thirteenth century, the poet known as Rumi expressed the deepest feelings of the heart through his poetry. This volume consists of new translations edited by Deepak Chopra to evoke the rich mood and music of Rumi's love poems. Exalted yearning, ravishing ecstasy, and consuming desire emerge from these poems as powerf</span>
  <span id="freeText4763168872098355559" style="display:none">Born Jalal ad-Din Mohammed Balkhi in Persia early in the thirteenth century, the poet known as Rumi expressed the deepest feelings of the heart through his poetry. This volume consists of new translations edited by Deepak Chopra to evoke the rich mood and music of Rumi's love poems. Exalted yearning, ravishing ecstasy, and consuming desire emerge from these poems as powerfully today as they did on their creation more than 700 years ago.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />'These poems reflect the deepest longings of the human heart as it searches for the divine. They celebrate love. Each poetic whisper is urgent, expressing the desire that penetrates human relationships and inspires intimacy with the self, silently nurturing an affinity for the Beloved. Both Fereydoun Kia, the translator, and I hope that you will share the experience of ravishing ecstasy that the poems of Rumi evoked in us. In this volume we have sought to capture in English the dreams, wishes, hopes, desires, and feelings of a Persian poet who continues to amaze, bewilder, confound, and teach, one thousand years after he walked on this earth' - Deepak Chopra</span>
  <a data-text-id="4763168872098355559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12316571669895358863">I think Rumi's poetry is unsurpassed in its mysticism and beauty.<br/><br/><i>"In your light I learn how to love.<br/>In your beauty, how to make poems."<br/><br/>"You dance inside my chest,<br/>where no one sees you,"<br/><br/>"but sometimes I do, and that<br/>sight becomes this art."</i> pg 62<br/><br/>When my husband and I were first dating, we lived in different parts of the country.   <span id="freeText12316571669895358863" style="display:none">I think Rumi's poetry is unsurpassed in its mysticism and beauty.<br /><br /><i>"In your light I learn how to love.<br />In your beauty, how to make poems."<br /><br />"You dance inside my chest,<br />where no one sees you,"<br /><br />"but sometimes I do, and that<br />sight becomes this art."</i> pg 62<br /><br />When my husband and I were first dating, we lived in different parts of the country. This was one of the books he sent when he found out that I loved poetry. He bought himself a copy too and we read it together- separately. Then we talked about it on the phone later. This was in the days before Skype or Facetime. It seems so quaint now.<br /><br /><i>"I desire you<br />more than food<br />or drink"<br /><br />"My body<br />my senses<br />my mind<br />hunger for your taste"<br /><br />"I can sense your presence<br />in my heart<br />although you belong<br />to all the world"<br /><br />"I wait<br />with silent passion<br />for one gesture<br />one glance<br />from you"</i> pg 34<br /><br />I'll be married for 11 years this May.<br /><br />If you ever meet someone romantically interesting who tells you they like poetry, buy them a book of Rumi. You won't regret it- at least, my husband says he doesn't. :)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3522759235084169440">Both light and shadow<br/>are the dance of Love.<br/><br/>Love has no cause;<br/>it is the astrolabe of God’s secrets.<br/><br/>Lover and Loving are inseparable<br/>and timeless.<br/> <br/>Although I may try to describe Love<br/>when I experience it I am speechless.<br/><br/>Although I may try to write about Love<br/>I am rendered helpless;<br/><br/>my pen breaks and the paper slips away<br/>at the ineffable place<br/>where Lover, Loving and Loved are one.<br/> <br/><br/>Every moment is made glorious<br/>by the light of Love.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/83139-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780609602430" href="/book/show/767549.The_Love_Poems_of_Rumi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83139-jennifer?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer347333234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer177907874971131734">So one night I got all hopped up on the idea of getting my poetry collection all entered into goodreads, so spent the evening transporting stacks of books from my shelves to my computer and back, entering ISBNs. Many books I spent some time flipping through, trying to remember if I'd read them all the way through, and if so, what I'd thought of them. This book was so short, I thought, "I should just read it now, and I'll know for sure."<br/><br/>This book was, sadly, a disappointment. It was Rumi, alright</span>
  <span id="freeText177907874971131734" style="display:none">So one night I got all hopped up on the idea of getting my poetry collection all entered into goodreads, so spent the evening transporting stacks of books from my shelves to my computer and back, entering ISBNs. Many books I spent some time flipping through, trying to remember if I'd read them all the way through, and if so, what I'd thought of them. This book was so short, I thought, "I should just read it now, and I'll know for sure."<br /><br />This book was, sadly, a disappointment. It was Rumi, alright, so there was wisdom, and irreverence, and reverence, and love. But the translations just didn't <i>sing</i> to me. They were too heavy, somehow. Just as I was thinking how much better I loved Coleman Barks's translations, I reached the end, where Chopra confesses how his work was inspired by Barks, and he includes a few of Barks's versions of examples and immediately I found them as moving as Chopra's versions weren't. These were cleaner, brighter, somehow. More fully realized in a modern poetic sensibility.<br /><br />Okay, that last sentence gave me the vapours. I need to go lie down. </span>
  <a data-text-id="177907874971131734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1264855349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1535173721475305969">Comentarios y fragmentos para el futuro. El amor es como un ladrón en la noche.<br/><br/><i>I- Sobre la alquimia como una forma de dialéctica. Elogio de un Dios alquimista.</i><br/><blockquote>You are the master alchemist.<br/><br/>You light the fire of love<br/>in earth and sky<br/>in heart and soul<br/>of every being.<br/><br/>Through your loving<br/>existence and nonexistence merge.<br/>All opposites unite.<br/>all that is profane<br/>becomes sacred again. (p. 19)</blockquote><br/><br/><i>II- El sueño es humo, el amor y Dios-en-el-cuerpo.</i><br/> <blockquote>You arouse with your touch<br/>although I can’t see your hands.<br/>You have </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1535173721475305969" style="display:none">Comentarios y fragmentos para el futuro. El amor es como un ladrón en la noche.<br /><br /><i>I- Sobre la alquimia como una forma de dialéctica. Elogio de un Dios alquimista.</i><br /><blockquote>You are the master alchemist.<br /><br />You light the fire of love<br />in earth and sky<br />in heart and soul<br />of every being.<br /><br />Through your loving<br />existence and nonexistence merge.<br />All opposites unite.<br />all that is profane<br />becomes sacred again. (p. 19)</blockquote><br /><br /><i>II- El sueño es humo, el amor y Dios-en-el-cuerpo.</i><br /> <blockquote>You arouse with your touch<br />although I can’t see your hands.<br />You have kissed me with tenderness<br />although I haven’t seen your lips.<br />You are hidden from me.<br /><br />But it is you who keeps me alive. (p. 22)</blockquote><br /><br /><i>III- La experiencia física (el baile/rito) como fuente de conocimiento y unión con lo otro (Dios/los amores/la esencia).</i><br /> <blockquote>In my hallucination<br />I saw my Beloved’s flower garden<br /><br />In my vertigo<br />in my dizziness<br />in my drunken haze<br />whirling and dancing<br />like a spinning wheel<br />I saw myself<br />as the source of existence<br /><br />I was there in the beginning<br />and I was the spirit of love<br /><br />Now I am sober<br />there is only hangover<br />and the memory of love<br />and only the sorrow (p.25) </blockquote><br /><br /><i>IV- <b>El amor hace dioses de los hombres.</b> Vitalismo. El enamorado y el bailarín como Superhombre nietzscheano:</i><br /> <blockquote>My friends have become strangers<br />and I’m surrounded by enemies<br />but I’m free as the wind<br />no longer hurt by those who reproach me<br /><br />I’m at home wherever I am<br />and in the room of lovers<br />I can see with closed eyes<br />the beauty that dances<br />behind the veils<br />intoxicated with love<br />I too dance the rhythm<br />of this moving world<br />I have lost my senses<br />in my world of lovers (p. 29) </blockquote><br /><br /><i>V- Desde El Corán: amar a una persona es amar a todo al Mundo. (Quienes se unan a sus esposas estarán más cerca de Allah.)</i><br /> <blockquote>I am ashamed<br />to call this love human<br />and afraid of God<br />to call it divine<br />(...)<br />My arrow of love<br />has arrived at the target<br />I am in the house of mercy<br />and my heart<br />is a place of prayer (p.47) </blockquote><br /><br /><i>VI- Crítica de la legalidad, elogio de una espiritualidad interior.</i><br /> <blockquote>I’m sick of mortal kings.<br />I long to see your light.<br />With lamps in hand<br />the sheikhs and mullahs roam<br />the dark alleys of these towns<br />not finding what they seek. (p.16)<br /><br />Cease looking for flowers!<br />There blooms a garden in your own home.<br />While you look for trinkets<br />the treasure house awaits you in your own being. (p. 48) </blockquote></span>

  <span id="freeText5929267442707576424" style="display:none">Poetry is something that suddenly arises in the mind and it's related to the surroundings of poet. Respected Rumi had shown the alchemy of love, the effects of love, how people thinks after falling in love.<br /> Likely- <br /><b>

People make their lovers happy because their happiness rely on theirs.<br /><br />Here comes respected Rumi to help me with these lines -<br /><b>

<div id="review_19692996" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19692996" />
    <a title="Peach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/971937-peach"><img alt="Peach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491025p2/971937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19692996?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peach" href="/user/show/971937-peach">Peach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19692996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7298979228336137634">I'm reading and re-reading love poems lately, as I'm getting married next month and have been on the hunt for the perfect reading for our ceremony. Rumi, who I always thought of, maybe only by stereotype and not really by truth, as a steady, sort of wise thinker who contemplatively and maturely crafts poems from this noble, truth-holding sort of place, is kind of crazy when it comes to love.<br/><br/>I'd hoped to find a reading that would capture the essence of love in a peaceful and harmonic state: witho</span>
  <span id="freeText7298979228336137634" style="display:none">I'm reading and re-reading love poems lately, as I'm getting married next month and have been on the hunt for the perfect reading for our ceremony. Rumi, who I always thought of, maybe only by stereotype and not really by truth, as a steady, sort of wise thinker who contemplatively and maturely crafts poems from this noble, truth-holding sort of place, is kind of crazy when it comes to love.<br /><br />I'd hoped to find a reading that would capture the essence of love in a peaceful and harmonic state: without frenzy and fury, without agony and desperation. Instead, with Rumi I've found drunken madness--his words, both of them. It seems that in Rumi's world, there is a division between being in love and the rest of life: "Because of your love / I have lost my sobriety / I am intoxicated / by the madness of love / In this fog / I have become a stranger to myself / I'm so drunk / I've lost the way to my house." <br /><br />I don't want to believe that being in love means you lose your home. In fact, personally, being so far from my physical home and making the decision to become part of another family, I want some sort of reassurance that committing to love does not separate one from one's original home. I also don't believe that being in love means you find your home--<br /><br />I believe, I guess (conviction, right?) that love, especially when it comes to marriage and partnership, is a partial blend. I maintain my "self"; he maintains his "self." And then we jointly make a third "self" which becomes the relationship between us.<br /><br />Rumi says to his sweetheart, "Your touch has filled me with desire / I am no longer separate from you ... Let me merge with you."<br /><br />Merge. Merge not. I actually think that I'm offering greater respect to my loved one if I do not merge with him, but rather, if I let him stand separate from me, and admire him, and support him, and adore him without actually becoming him. We are stronger this way, as two separate people joining forces, rather than simply folding into one.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7298979228336137634" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I recall reading one of his poems as a child but these beautiful gems are so enthralling and intense, I felt a tugging at my heart as I read of the love Rumi feels for his beloved.<br/><br/>The poems have a mystical feel which is so engrossing and entertaining. These are poems I will read again and again because I will never tire of reading them or deriving blissful enjoyment from them.<br/><br/>Rumi is a master poet.</span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143211907" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2903115063042173285">THE LOVE POEMS OF RUMI WAS A FANTASTIC POETRY BOOK BY DEEPAK CHOPRA. THE POEMS WERE ALL ABOUT LOVE WHICH HIS TITLE PROVES BUT ALSO ABOUT DIFFERENT RELATIONSHIPS WITH OTHERS THAT ARE IN YOUR LIFE OR ACTUALLY YOUR LOVER.<br/>THE TYPE OF POET DEEPAK IS, IS WISE VERY OPEN MINDED AND MAKES THE READER FEEL WHAT HE IS WRITING ABOUT THROUGH HIS WORDS. THE AUTHOR MAKES A READER THINK ABOUT THEIR MISTAKES OR THEIR GOOD TIMES IN A DIFFERENT MATTER HE WOULD BREAK IT DOWN SO THAT ANYONE THAT WAS READING HIS POETR</span>
  <span id="freeText2903115063042173285" style="display:none">THE LOVE POEMS OF RUMI WAS A FANTASTIC POETRY BOOK BY DEEPAK CHOPRA. THE POEMS WERE ALL ABOUT LOVE WHICH HIS TITLE PROVES BUT ALSO ABOUT DIFFERENT RELATIONSHIPS WITH OTHERS THAT ARE IN YOUR LIFE OR ACTUALLY YOUR LOVER.<br />THE TYPE OF POET DEEPAK IS, IS WISE VERY OPEN MINDED AND MAKES THE READER FEEL WHAT HE IS WRITING ABOUT THROUGH HIS WORDS. THE AUTHOR MAKES A READER THINK ABOUT THEIR MISTAKES OR THEIR GOOD TIMES IN A DIFFERENT MATTER HE WOULD BREAK IT DOWN SO THAT ANYONE THAT WAS READING HIS POETRY COULD LEARN NEW SUGGESTIONS FOR DIFFERENT SITUATIONS. THE PASSION DEEPAK PUTS THROUGH WORDS TOUCHES YOU AS IF YOU HAVE BEEN IN THAT SPECIFIC SITUATION BUT HAS A READER IMAGINE BEING IN THAT CIRCLE OF SITUATIONS THE AUTHOR HAS ALOT TO SAY ABOUT LOVE AND IF A READER HAS BEEN IN THAT SURROUNDENCE HE OR SHE COULD RELATE TO DEEPAKS POETS WITHOUT BEING LOST OR NOT WANTING TO CONTINUE READING HIS POETS.THE WAY THE POET DESCIBES HIS POETRY MAKES A READER PICK UP ANOTHER ONE OF HIS BOOKS BECAUSE HE GIVES GREAT DESCIPTION IN HIS POEMS TO SUPPORT AND GIVE EVIDENCE TO EACH OF HIS TITLE. SO FOR THE RATE OF THIS BOOK WOULD B FIVE STARS AND I WOULD RECOMMEND ANYONE WHO IS OR IS NOT INTO POETRY SHOULD TRY TO READ DEEPAK CHOPRA`S POETRY BOOKS.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2903115063042173285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3659567284031832546">I should have known anything Deepak Chopra put his name to would be drivel. It's like the bad poetry of a repressed teenager. I feel this and I feel that, blah blah blah. Free-association in all the wrong places. It does however come across as sincere (and, mercifully, terse).<br/><br/>I also feel like Rumi was not making full use of his time machine by using it to name several of his poems after 20th century pop hits, such as "Looking For Love", "Desire" and "Do You Love Me?".<br/><br/>Hold up! Before you go...I a</span>
  <span id="freeText3659567284031832546" style="display:none">I should have known anything Deepak Chopra put his name to would be drivel. It's like the bad poetry of a repressed teenager. I feel this and I feel that, blah blah blah. Free-association in all the wrong places. It does however come across as sincere (and, mercifully, terse).<br /><br />I also feel like Rumi was not making full use of his time machine by using it to name several of his poems after 20th century pop hits, such as "Looking For Love", "Desire" and "Do You Love Me?".<br /><br />Hold up! Before you go...I admit I found this poem-ender very beautiful:<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>All that is profane<br />Becomes sacred again.</blockquote>
</i></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/775543186" />
    <a title="Charlie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3532958-charlie"><img alt="Charlie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534124001p2/3532958.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/775543186?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlie" href="/user/show/3532958-charlie">Charlie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780609602430" href="/book/show/767549.The_Love_Poems_of_Rumi">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer775543186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4904618962641962442">It frustrates me how much more attention is given in this book to Deepak Chopra, a man who profits off other people's spirituality, than to the thirteenth-century Persian poet who wrote the works Chopra collected here. I also think the poems chosen were a little repetitive, talking on almost every other page about "fiery passion". Maybe this was just an act of bad translating. Some of the poems were really nice, despite knowing they'd likely be better in their original language. One quote I like</span>
  <span id="freeText4904618962641962442" style="display:none">It frustrates me how much more attention is given in this book to Deepak Chopra, a man who profits off other people's spirituality, than to the thirteenth-century Persian poet who wrote the works Chopra collected here. I also think the poems chosen were a little repetitive, talking on almost every other page about "fiery passion". Maybe this was just an act of bad translating. Some of the poems were really nice, despite knowing they'd likely be better in their original language. One quote I liked: "Oh, Sweet Bitterness! I will soothe you and heal you. I will bring you roses. I, too, have been covered with thorns."</span>
  <a data-text-id="4904618962641962442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('aotqsbBWxZLUK0+nisp+YqlwjUKdj7FBwI99ZqjhzjluqK0aDy2mNzShApH5uM6pGdz4Y0l346rLBpsn2mkl3A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jNlU3IyBHOLL0tZglRQlA/XtyPRawmcMRixDA7q23reI+pN3M/p/RytYm1bmZpXIRUG91Y46NedNpaVCyD41Ug==')})
  }

After working on a farm, in a steel mill, as a window washer, as a salesman, and at various other jobs, he entered Ohio University in 1931, and in due course was graduated. He then spent five years as a social investigator in New York. During the second World War he served in the United States Army, being commissioned a lieutenant of the Signal Corps in the autumn of 1942 and later being promoted to a captaincy. After the war he returned to New York and devoted his time to writing.This Is My Beloved, the remarkable diary in verse that has become one of the most popular books of poetry, was his first published volume, though his work was already familiar to readers of Poetry, Fantasy, the Yale Review, and the New Republic. The state of God-Realisation is symbolised through union with a Beloved, and drinking the wine of spiritual love.\n\nThis compact version of the Divan of Hafez is a facsimile illuminated manuscript, complete with beautiful Persian calligraphy and miniature illustrations. There are 43 ghazals, translated into English by classical scholar Gertrude Bell. It is a truly beautiful introduction not only to the works of this beloved Sufi mystic, but also to the artistry of Mahmoud Farshchian. It is like getting two books in one: poetry and art.\n\n&quot;Hafiz has no peer.&quot; — Goethe\nPoetry is the greatest literary form of ancient Persia and modern Iran, and the fourteenth-century poet known as Hafiz is its preeminent master. Little is known about the poet\'s life, and there are more legends than facts relating to the particulars of his existence. This mythic quality is entirely appropriate for the man known as &quot;The Interpreter of Mysteries&quot; and &quot;The Tongue of the Hidden,&quot; whose verse is regarded as oracular by those seeking guidance and attempting to realize wishes.\nA mere fraction of what is presumed to have been an extensive body of work survives. This collection is derived from Hafiz\'s Divan (collected poems), a classic of Sufism. The short poems, called ghazals, are sonnet-like arrangements of varied numbers of couplets. In the tradition of Persian poetry and Sufi philosophy, each poem corresponds to two interpretations, sensual and mystic.\nThis outstanding translation of Hafiz\'s poetry was created by historian and Arabic scholar Gertrude Bell, who observed, &quot;These are the utterances of a great poet, the imaginative interpreter of the heart of man; they are not of one age, or of another, but for all       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/875661.Rumi">About Rumi</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/875661.Rumi"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1217355543p3/875661.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/875661.Rumi">Rumi
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
12,711
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11111112893097997303">Jalāl ad-Dīn Muhammad Rūmī - also known as Jalāl ad-Dīn Muhammad Balkhī, Mevlânâ/Mawlānā (مولانا, "our master"), Mevlevî/Mawlawī (مولوی, "my master") and more popularly simply as Rumi - was a 13th-century Persian poet, jurist, Islamic scholar, theologian and Sufi mystic who lived in Konya, a city of Ottoman Empire (Today's Turkey). His poems have been widely translated into many of the world's lan</span>
  <span id="freeText11111112893097997303" style="display:none">Jalāl ad-Dīn Muhammad Rūmī - also known as Jalāl ad-Dīn Muhammad Balkhī, Mevlânâ/Mawlānā (مولانا, "our master"), Mevlevî/Mawlawī (مولوی, "my master") and more popularly simply as Rumi - was a 13th-century Persian poet, jurist, Islamic scholar, theologian and Sufi mystic who lived in Konya, a city of Ottoman Empire (Today's Turkey). His poems have been widely translated into many of the world's languages, and he has been described as the most popular poet and the best-selling poet in the United States.<br /><br />His poetry has influenced Persian literature, but also Turkish, Ottoman Turkish, Azerbaijani, Punjabi, Hindi, and Urdu, as well as the literature of some other Turkic, Iranian, and Indo-Aryan languages including Chagatai, Pashto, and Bengali.<br /><br />Due to quarrels between different dynasties in Khorāṣān, opposition to the Khwarizmid Shahs who were considered devious by his father, Bahā ud-Dīn Wālad or fear of the impending Mongol cataclysm, his father decided to migrate westwards, eventually settling in the Anatolian city Konya, where he lived most of his life, composed one of the crowning glories of Persian literature, and profoundly affected the culture of the area.<br /><br />When his father died, Rumi, aged 25, inherited his position as the head of an Islamic school. One of Baha' ud-Din's students, Sayyed Burhan ud-Din Muhaqqiq Termazi, continued to train Rumi in the Shariah as well as the Tariqa, especially that of Rumi's father. For nine years, Rumi practised Sufism as a disciple of Burhan ud-Din until the latter died in 1240 or 1241. Rumi's public life then began: he became an Islamic Jurist, issuing fatwas and giving sermons in the mosques of Konya. He also served as a Molvi (Islamic teacher) and taught his adherents in the madrassa. During this period, Rumi also travelled to Damascus and is said to have spent four years there.<br /><br />It was his meeting with the dervish Shams-e Tabrizi on 15 November 1244 that completely changed his life. From an accomplished teacher and jurist, Rumi was transformed into an ascetic.<br /><br />On the night of 5 December 1248, as Rumi and Shams were talking, Shams was called to the back door. He went out, never to be seen again. Rumi's love for, and his bereavement at the death of, Shams found their expression in an outpouring of lyric poems, Divan-e Shams-e Tabrizi. He himself went out searching for Shams and journeyed again to Damascus.<br /><br />Rumi found another companion in Salaḥ ud-Din-e Zarkub, a goldsmith. After Salah ud-Din's death, Rumi's scribe and favourite student, Hussam-e Chalabi, assumed the role of Rumi's companion. Hussam implored Rumi to write more. Rumi spent the next 12 years of his life in Anatolia dictating the six volumes of this masterwork, the Masnavi, to Hussam.<br /><br />In December 1273, Rumi fell ill and died on the 17th of December in Konya.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11111112893097997303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1292839-the-love-poems-of-rumi">Quotes from The Love Poems of...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You are the Essence of the Essence,<br />The intoxication of Love.<br />I long to sing Your Praises<br />but stand mute<br />with the agony of wishing in my heart !&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/431064-you-are-the-essence-of-the-essence-the-intoxication-of">89 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Looking for Your Face<br /><br />From the beginning of my life<br />I have been looking for your face<br />but today I have seen it<br /><br />Today I have seen<br />the charm, the beauty,<br />the unfathomable grace<br />of the face<br />that I was looking for<br /><br />Today I have found you<br />and those who laughed<br />and scorned me yesterday<br />are sorry that they were not looking<br />as I did<br /><br />I am bewildered by the magnificence<br />of your beauty<br />and wish to see you<br />with a hundred eyes<br /><br />My heart has burned with passion<br />and has searched forever<br />for this wondrous beauty<br />that I now behold<br /><br />I am ashamed<br />to call this love human<br />and afraid of God<br />to call it divine<br /><br />Your fragrant breath<br />like the morning breeze<br />has come to the stillness of the garden<br />You have breathed new life into me<br />I have become your sunshine<br />and also your shadow<br /><br />My soul is screaming in ecstasy<br />Every fiber of my being<br />is in love with you<br /><br />Your effulgence<br />has lit a fire in my heart<br />and you have made radiant<br />for me<br />the earth and sky<br /><br />My arrow of love<br />has arrived at the target<br />I am in the house of mercy<br />and my heart<br />is a place of prayer&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
